    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12516162113670596909">Dickinson’s poetry is remarkable for its tightly controlled emotional and intellectual energy. The longest poem covers less than two pages. Yet in theme and tone her writing reaches for the sublime as it charts the landscape of the human soul. A true innovator, Dickinson experimented freely with conventional rhythm and meter, and often used dashes, off rhymes, and unusual </span>
  <span id="freeText12516162113670596909" style="display:none">Dickinson’s poetry is remarkable for its tightly controlled emotional and intellectual energy. The longest poem covers less than two pages. Yet in theme and tone her writing reaches for the sublime as it charts the landscape of the human soul. A true innovator, Dickinson experimented freely with conventional rhythm and meter, and often used dashes, off rhymes, and unusual metaphors—techniques that strongly influenced modern poetry. Dickinson’s idiosyncratic style, along with her deep resonance of thought and her observations about life and death, love and nature, and solitude and society, have firmly established her as one of America’s true poetic geniuses.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12516162113670596909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_945060909" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/945060909" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/945060909?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=best-words-best-order">best-words-best-order</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer945060909" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4744992786768518007"><blockquote>Can you write a book review<br/>Entirely in verse?<br/>Omitting standard sentences<br/>For stanzas taut and terse?<br/><br/>It seems a fitting treatment<br/>For such a book as this;<br/>So humor me, I beg you—<br/>And my limited wit.<br/><br/>Emily Dickinson was a poet,<br/>One of the very best;<br/>A natural gift with language—<br/>At once daft and deft.<br/><br/>Something of a recluse,<br/>Something of a crank;<br/>Living closed up in her room—<br/>Like a fish in a tank.<br/><br/>Undoubtedly a genius,<br/>Ahead of her time;<br/>Unappreciated in her life,<br/>For her erratic rhymes.<br/><br/>But when she finally pass</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4744992786768518007" style="display:none"><blockquote>Can you write a book review<br />Entirely in verse?<br />Omitting standard sentences<br />For stanzas taut and terse?<br /><br />It seems a fitting treatment<br />For such a book as this;<br />So humor me, I beg you—<br />And my limited wit.<br /><br />Emily Dickinson was a poet,<br />One of the very best;<br />A natural gift with language—<br />At once daft and deft.<br /><br />Something of a recluse,<br />Something of a crank;<br />Living closed up in her room—<br />Like a fish in a tank.<br /><br />Undoubtedly a genius,<br />Ahead of her time;<br />Unappreciated in her life,<br />For her erratic rhymes.<br /><br />But when she finally passed away,<br />Her cache of poems was found;<br />Edited to the day’s tastes—<br />The dashes taken out.<br /><br />The dash—the perfect punctuation<br />For her unique style;<br />Jagged—ragged—sudden—striking<br />And also—versatile.<br /><br />Obsessed with life—and death—and bees,<br />Most of her poems are short;<br />Some of them only one quatrain,<br />They end before they start.<br /><br />And what entrancing rhythm!<br />Like the beating of a drum—<br />Her words hammer forward—<br />Marching—stomping—thumping—done!<br /><br />The classic case of genius,<br />At first misunderstood;<br />Now her poems are classic,<br />Widely read and widely loved.<br /><br />So thank you, Ms. Dickinson,<br />For dedicating yourself—<br />To art, to words, to poetry—<br />To posterity’s bookshelf.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="4744992786768518007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1148700761?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1148700761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18298616192829104605">Abstract and intellectual, Emily Dickinson's work echoes the concerns of seventeenth-century metaphysical poetry: her short poems address religion and morality, love and death, hope and despair, through inventive metaphors and perplexing paradoxes. In contrast to her literary antecedents, though, Dickinson's language reads as a great deal more precise and less self-indulgent. So, too, do her poems tend to resist visualization altogether, whereas the metaphysical poets' work simply features stran</span>
  <span id="freeText18298616192829104605" style="display:none">Abstract and intellectual, Emily Dickinson's work echoes the concerns of seventeenth-century metaphysical poetry: her short poems address religion and morality, love and death, hope and despair, through inventive metaphors and perplexing paradoxes. In contrast to her literary antecedents, though, Dickinson's language reads as a great deal more precise and less self-indulgent. So, too, do her poems tend to resist visualization altogether, whereas the metaphysical poets' work simply features strange, quasi-mystical images. Dickinson's brevity makes her work accessible even to readers who typically dislike poetry. Still, the complexity of her thought, as well as her engagement with the major issues of her time, invites careful readings of her poems, be they focused on form or history or both.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18298616192829104605" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3036311825" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517362426" href="/book/show/303865.Collected_Poems_of_Emily_Dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3036311825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer856515861607162187">Aside from the few poems here and there, this is the first time I've read a proper collection of Dickinson poems, and it's easy to see why she is just so popular. Her poetry really does take you away from the hustle and bustle of life, and I was left in a complete state of tranquil bliss as I worked my through the wonderful poems on offer. It was like sitting in a meadow not an apartment. This volume is spilt into four parts - Life, Love, Nature, and Time & Eternity, and it's so difficult to pic</span>
  <span id="freeText856515861607162187" style="display:none">Aside from the few poems here and there, this is the first time I've read a proper collection of Dickinson poems, and it's easy to see why she is just so popular. Her poetry really does take you away from the hustle and bustle of life, and I was left in a complete state of tranquil bliss as I worked my through the wonderful poems on offer. It was like sitting in a meadow not an apartment. This volume is spilt into four parts - Life, Love, Nature, and Time &amp; Eternity, and it's so difficult to pick the stand out poems because there were just so many of them! While some of the poems felt like spontaneous flashes of insight unrelated to her outward circumstances, others clearly came on a more personal level. Had it not been for Dickinson's sister Lavinia we may well have never got to read the hundreds of poems Emily wrote in her lifetime. It's sad to think she was initially rejected, but the fact she had no publication pressures or restrictions, left her free to continue to write in an unhampered and original style.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="856515861607162187" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2837989823978078892">The major characteristics of the poems: <br/>• <i>
  <b>Theme and Tone</b>
</i><br/>• <i>
  <b>Form and Style</b>
</i><br/>• <i>
  <b>Meter and Rhyme</b>
</i><br/>• <i>
  <b>Punctuation and Syntax</b>
</i><br/>• <i>
  <b>Diction</b>
</i><br/>It has literally taken days for me to go through deepening verses thoroughly to acquire the slightest portion of them straight to the heart. <br/>A moment of contact when the thriving elixir hits your system lofting you into an era where you are beyond the field of right or wrong, consciousness or sub-consciousness, light or shadow!<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1518446749ra/25046721.gif' width='500' height='470' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>You feel the phrases shaping </span>
  <span id="freeText2837989823978078892" style="display:none">The major characteristics of the poems: <br>• <i>
  <b>Theme and Tone</b>
</i><br>• <i>
  <b>Form and Style</b>
</i><br>• <i>
  <b>Meter and Rhyme</b>
</i><br>• <i>
  <b>Punctuation and Syntax</b>

</i><br>It has literally taken days for me to go through deepening verses thoroughly to acquire the slightest portion of them straight to the heart. <br>A moment of contact when the thriving elixir hits your system lofting you into an era where you are beyond the field of right or wrong, consciousness or sub-consciousness, light or shadow!<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1518446749ra/25046721.gif" width="500" height="470" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>You feel the phrases shaping somewhere inside your head bottling up to be released at the moment of salvation. <br>Emily’s poems are literally the soul food. They reflect beyond the death grips, the facts of life which human beings find hard to deal with always – <b>Life – Love – Death</b>. (**Though the <b>Eternity</b> section was the most alluring one!) <br><i>Emily Dickinson scrutinized everything with clear-eyed frankness.</i> <br>Indeed it is the ultimate truth. Few of verses are really absurd in a way that they linger in your mind on the loop.<br>The editors (Emily's friends) have composed her poems into four categories:<br>- <b>Life</b><br>- <b>Love</b><br>- <b>Nature</b><br>- <b>Time and Eternity</b> <br>Her seasonal transition is totally phenomenal reminds me of:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1518446749i/25046722._SY540_.jpg" width="500" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Bequest<br> You left me, sweet, two legacies, —<br> A legacy of love<br> A Heavenly Father would content,<br> Had He the offer of;<br> You left me boundaries of pain<br> Capacious as the sea,<br> Between eternity and time,<br> Your consciousness and me.</i> <br>- The controversial truth stated simply flat. Love and pain bound together! <br><i>Love<br> Love is anterior to life,<br> Posterior to death,<br> Initial of creation, and<br> The exponent of breath.</i><br>P.S.: It will take time to dive in her phrases, to understand the shallowness as well as there depth! The journey will lead to the destination where everything is meaningfully fine and justified! </span>

<div id="review_1283833774" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41289357-maica?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1283833774" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12986140523777564444"> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463198111i/19092013._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='304' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Emily Dickinson's poetry is as subtle and delicate as how she lived her life. <br/><br/>Imagine a life spent in total seclusion from society and the outside world, as how she lived: and yet her ideas are richer and profound compared to those exposed to society. Perhaps, in isolation within her own world and nature (and judging from her poems, she must have been an avid history and literary enthusiast), the themes found on her poems attained a unique kind of message: subtle and gentle, lofty, and even sat</span>
  <span id="freeText12986140523777564444" style="display:none"> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1463198111i/19092013._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="304" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Emily Dickinson's poetry is as subtle and delicate as how she lived her life. <br><br>Imagine a life spent in total seclusion from society and the outside world, as how she lived: and yet her ideas are richer and profound compared to those exposed to society. Perhaps, in isolation within her own world and nature (and judging from her poems, she must have been an avid history and literary enthusiast), the themes found on her poems attained a unique kind of message: subtle and gentle, lofty, and even satirical at times.<br><br>I like the way she deals with the topic of death, loss, love, justice, and eternity. Her style is not confined to the constraints of formal poetic construction, and yet she achieves the richness of imagery and metaphorical representation. <br><br><blockquote>
  <i>because I could not stop for death<br>he kindly stopped for me<br>the carriage held but just ourselves<br>and immortality<i></i></i>
</blockquote><br>One thing which might have greatly influenced her writing and the themes found on her poetry is her ambiguous relationship with the editor of this current collection, TW Higginson, who must have served as her only contact within the literary world.<br><br>I recommend reading the biography and beautiful poetry of Emily Dickinson, a maiden, dubbed as 'an eccentric recluse', who closed her life from the outside world.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1432176064i/14907353._SY540_.jpg" width="427" height="640" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_2783390616" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2783390616" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2783390616?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=american-lit">american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2783390616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14290525763927503235">This collection consists of a considerable amount of poems written by Emily Dickinson. She is a posthumously celebrated poet, whose poems were unknown to the world. Even her family knew nothing about them till her death. <br/><br/>According to the biographical information, Emily Dickinson had lived a solitary life. Her poetry is a reflection of a secluded thinker. Many of the poems in the collection are prone to different interpretation, according to the intellect of the reader. That shows how clever her </span>
  <span id="freeText14290525763927503235" style="display:none">This collection consists of a considerable amount of poems written by Emily Dickinson. She is a posthumously celebrated poet, whose poems were unknown to the world. Even her family knew nothing about them till her death. <br /><br />According to the biographical information, Emily Dickinson had lived a solitary life. Her poetry is a reflection of a secluded thinker. Many of the poems in the collection are prone to different interpretation, according to the intellect of the reader. That shows how clever her poems are. And it also shows an inner depth that was held so secretively by the poet, for it is difficult to fully discern many of the poems. <br /><br />Most of the poems are short, and consisted of an erratic rhyme which was not appreciated during the 19th century. Many of them show an outward simplicity; but they have an insight beyond the superficial exterior. Some are so deep that it is very difficult to fathom the poet's perception. <br /><br />In my reading life, this is the first time I read such unusual classical poetry; unusual in the sense of form, colour, character and tone. There is no doubt that Emily Dickinson is an intellect and a gifted poet. But she was far ahead of her time. Perhaps she knew it. She tried the water with few of her poems, and when they were not accepted for publication, she didn't attempt at publication and wrote for herself. <br /><br />This collection categorizes the poems under different headings including, life, nature and love. Thematically, they touch on love, life, death, soul, religion and morals among others. I enjoyed many of them, including some of those I couldn't fully understand. What was really amazing is Emily Dickinson's ability to take you on to a different plane. It is not always picturesque, but is almost always serene. Her poems sooth you and calm you.<br /><br />As I already said, there were many poems that I enjoyed; but I won't crowd my review by sharing them all here. There is however one simple poem under the category of love that touched me deep; so as a tribute to the poet as well as a token of my appreciation of her, I would share just that one. <br /><br /><i>"Heart, we will forget him!<br /> You and I, tonight!<br /> You may forget the warmth he gave,<br /> I will forget the light.<br /><br /> When you have done, pray tell me,<br /> That I my thoughts may dim;<br /> Haste! lest while you're lagging,<br /> I may remember him! </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="14290525763927503235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_728472781" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/728472781" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17433080-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420185854p2/17433080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/728472781?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/17433080-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17433080-steve?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer728472781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5806732389690308235"><b> Preamble </b> (to be skipped)<br/><br/>I've been reading some poetry reviews by readers who are evidently lovers of prose, not poetry. Here are some ramblings motivated by those reviews. <br/><br/>Poetic prose is very admirable; prosodic poetry is not. It is very, very, very difficult to write a good love poem, because there are so many ways to fall into <i> cliché </i> and so few ways to startle, to reveal something unexpected - so difficult that most love poems are failures as poems, as it appears to me. (They may be succe</span>
  <span id="freeText5806732389690308235" style="display:none"><b> Preamble </b> (to be skipped)<br /><br />I've been reading some poetry reviews by readers who are evidently lovers of prose, not poetry. Here are some ramblings motivated by those reviews. <br /><br />Poetic prose is very admirable; prosodic poetry is not. It is very, very, very difficult to write a good love poem, because there are so many ways to fall into <i> cliché </i> and so few ways to startle, to reveal something unexpected - so difficult that most love poems are failures as poems, as it appears to me. (They may be successes in some other sense.) In my view, politics and poetry almost never mix well; political poems strike me almost always as rhetoric, not as poetry. <br /><br />I highly esteem brevity and distillation; I enjoy hints and gestures at complexes of meaning beyond the words actually employed; I appreciate dense, shifting clouds of meaning trying to adumbrate the non-simplicity inherent in so many aspects of life; but I also enjoy sharp, clear takes on that which is (relatively) simple; I don't care much for pretty filigree, because I prefer to see the language shuddering under the strain of the load it is bearing; I love the unexpected phrase/view/standpoint about the most quotidian and the most abstruse, but <i> nouvelle </i> for the sake of new gets old fast; I do not reflexively shout "obscurity, pretension, obfuscation" because I didn't understand the first time through. But that's just me... On with the review.<br /><br /><b> Review </b><br /><br />I have not yet read one of the biographies of Emily Dickinson (1830-1886), but I know how, disappointed by a few interactions with publishers, she made no further attempts; how she wrote nearly two thousand poems, discovered upon her death; how she rarely left the family house after she returned from college and, not long thereafter, rarely left her room. But I don't know the why of it.<br /><br />Whatever the reasons may be, what is clear is that the primary topics of these many poems are pain, fear, love, death and immortality. The pain that so occupied her is not physical pain, but the mental and emotional pain caused to a possibly over-sensitive, timid person both by other people and by herself.<br /><br />Her poems are typically short - few lines, short lines - and, to my mind, her best poems are intense, rugged, jagged in rhythm, with rhymes which appear to be accidental or so approximate that "near rhyme" just doesn't capture it. Granted, there are poems which strike me as exemplars of 19th century American "right thinking." I am allergic to "right thinking" of any stripe, but particularly the 19th century American variety gives me a rash.(*) So it's likely that those poems don't get a fair shake from me. You might like them better than I.<br /><br />Here are some of the poems I thought were remarkable, for one reason or another.<br /><br /><blockquote><br />I had no time to hate, because<br />The grave would hinder me,<br />And life was not so ample I<br />Could finish enmity.<br /><br />Nor had I time to love; but since<br />Some industry must be,<br />The little toil of love, I thought,<br />Was large enough for me.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Here is another mark of that toil of love. Note the sprung rhythm and missed rhymes in the first and last stanzas, while the middle three are almost regular in both rhyme and rhythm. In the mid-nineteenth century the poem would be judged a clumsy failure. But, to me, it is a fascinating little machine; and the content - she is speaking from experience here...<br /><br /><blockquote><br />One need not be a chamber to be haunted,<br />One need not be a house;<br />The brain has corridors surpassing<br />Material place.<br /><br />Far safer, of a midnight meeting<br />External ghost,<br />Than an interior confronting<br />That whiter host.<br /><br />Far safer through an Abbey gallop,<br />the stones achase,<br />Than, moonless, one's own self encounter<br />In lonesome place.<br /><br />Ourself, behind ourself concealed,<br />Should startle most;<br />Assassin, hid in our apartment,<br />Be horror's least.<br /><br />The prudent carries a revolver,<br />He bolts the door,<br />O'erlooking a superior spectre<br />More near.<br /><br /></blockquote><br /><br />So many little surprises; so much to occupy the active mind! It also hints at the "why" alluded to above. Her struggles also led her to poems like this one:<br /><br /><blockquote><br />The heart asks pleasure first,<br />And then, excuse from pain;<br />And then, those little anodynes<br />That deaden suffering;<br /><br />And then, to go to sleep;<br />And then, if it should be<br />The will of its Inquisitor,<br />The liberty to die.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />Two of my favorite poets, Paul Celan (1920-1970) and Georg Trakl (1887-1914), wrote darker poems, but Celan witnessed the Second World War and the German extermination camps (Celan survived, but his parents did not) and ultimately drowned himself in the Seine; and Trakl was a drug addict at 15, probably schizophrenic, and finally a medic in the front lines, broken in the first year of the First World War, dead of a cocaine overdose at 27. What agonies drew this poem out of Dickinson?<br /><br />I've had this book in my shelves for 17 years; I wish I had read it earlier.<br /><br />(*) So what of the 21st century Americans trying to resurrect 19th century American "right thinking"? Better not go there...</span>
  <a data-text-id="5806732389690308235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1683267136" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1683267136" />
    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447215825p2/48502194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1683267136?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/48502194-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517362426" href="/book/show/303865.Collected_Poems_of_Emily_Dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=2017-challenges">2017-challenges</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48502194-kathleen?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1683267136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9574743486046081515">Emily Dickinson was a recluse, and described as “well-behaved,” so it isn’t surprising that she hides things, that we find unsaid paragraphs behind her dashes, philosophies beneath her capital letters. It’s as if she wrote poetry as a kind of shorthand to herself.<br/><br/>The range is baffling—from silly to surreal to stunning. I read along, sometimes five or ten poems that did nothing for me (particularly the nature poems), and then bam! I was hit with something so unusual it stopped my breath. For exam</span>
  <span id="freeText9574743486046081515" style="display:none">Emily Dickinson was a recluse, and described as “well-behaved,” so it isn’t surprising that she hides things, that we find unsaid paragraphs behind her dashes, philosophies beneath her capital letters. It’s as if she wrote poetry as a kind of shorthand to herself.<br /><br />The range is baffling—from silly to surreal to stunning. I read along, sometimes five or ten poems that did nothing for me (particularly the nature poems), and then bam! I was hit with something so unusual it stopped my breath. For example, in “It ceased to hurt me, though so slow,” she describes being stuck in grief:<br /><br /><i>… that nestled close<br />As needles—ladies softly press<br />To Cushions Cheeks—<br />To keep their place—</i><br /><br />Grief nestled close, stuck to her like a needle in a pin cushion! … !! … !!! (That’s me, speechless.)<br /><br />I came away with many favorites. Prior to reading this, the one I liked best was “Tell all the Truth but tell it slant--,” which I still love but it has moved down the list a bit now, after “I started Early—Took my Dog,” with its beautiful description of being enveloped by the surf.<br /><br />And in the fanciful “Make me a picture of the sun,” she evokes the power of make-believe and dreaming:<br /><br /><i>Say if it’s really—warm at noon—<br />Whether it’s Buttercups—that “skim”—<br />Or Butterflies—that “bloom”?</i><br /><br />We can choose to see things differently if we wish. Emily certainly did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9574743486046081515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I've heard a lot about her, of course, and found that, like many others, I loved her poetry. <br/><br/>Her writing and style are unusual and spellbinding, the atmosphere - serene, ethereal, full of musing and silence, the themes - nature, death, spirituality. Most of the poems I read out loud multiple times before moving on to the next one, and most I wanted to bookmark. <br/><br/>I'm currently reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10663397.Emily_Dickinson_Letters' title='Emily Dickinson Letters by Emily Dickinson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Emily Dickinson: Letters</a>, and plan to read another collection of D</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Speranza" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Speranza" href="/user/show/13631093-speranza">Speranza</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1666161607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4579748731278442947">I read Emily Dickinson in translation back at school and remember thinking her poetry was plain.<br/>Reading her now, I realise that the plain one was me.<br/><br/>This, to me, is poetry in its purest and therefore most powerful form. <br/><br/>It is melody, it is painting, it is wisdom. It floats high above and it goes deep within. Simply beautiful.<br/><br/>I especially loved the nature poems. They are invigoratingly alive and they made me want to go out and run barefoot, hug a tree, get stung by a bee and burnt by the sun. To </span>
  <span id="freeText4579748731278442947" style="display:none">I read Emily Dickinson in translation back at school and remember thinking her poetry was plain.<br />Reading her now, I realise that the plain one was me.<br /><br />This, to me, is poetry in its purest and therefore most powerful form. <br /><br />It is melody, it is painting, it is wisdom. It floats high above and it goes deep within. Simply beautiful.<br /><br />I especially loved the nature poems. They are invigoratingly alive and they made me want to go out and run barefoot, hug a tree, get stung by a bee and burnt by the sun. To just for a moment feel that the substance running in my veins is blood and not internet connection.<br /></span>

<div id="review_3384608109" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3384608109" />
    <a title="Nadine Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115626-nadine-jones"><img alt="Nadine Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180985415p2/115626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3384608109?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nadine Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nadine Jones" href="/user/show/115626-nadine-jones">Nadine Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517362426" href="/book/show/303865.Collected_Poems_of_Emily_Dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115626-nadine-jones?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3384608109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13674380998931770523">I spent much of my high school and college years reading poetry, sometimes the classics, and sometimes just a random book I picked up at the library. At some point I decided that Emily Dickinson was one of my favorites. I mean, <i>I felt a funeral in my brain</i> and <i>Because I could not stop for death</i> and <i>I’m Nobody! Who are you?</i> are unrivaled, right? (Especially for a moody teen!!) <b>I still think those poems are unrivaled. </b> But now that I’m all old and crotchety, I’ve discovered that many of Dickinson’</span>
  <span id="freeText13674380998931770523" style="display:none">I spent much of my high school and college years reading poetry, sometimes the classics, and sometimes just a random book I picked up at the library. At some point I decided that Emily Dickinson was one of my favorites. I mean, <i>I felt a funeral in my brain</i> and <i>Because I could not stop for death</i> and <i>I’m Nobody! Who are you?</i> are unrivaled, right? (Especially for a moody teen!!) <b>I still think those poems are unrivaled. </b> But now that I’m all old and crotchety, I’ve discovered that many of Dickinson’s poems are just too fussy for my taste. <br /><br />There is still magic here, though. I enjoyed reading this collection, rediscovering old favorites, and appreciating other poems that I had forgotten.<br /><br /><blockquote><b> A narrow Fellow in the Grass</b><br />A narrow Fellow in the Grass<br />Occasionally rides -<br />You may have met him? Did you not<br />His notice instant is -<br /><br />The Grass divides as with a Comb,<br />A spotted Shaft is seen,<br />And then it closes at your Feet<br />And opens further on -<br /><br />He likes a Boggy Acre - <br />A Floor too cool for Corn -<br />But when a Boy and Barefoot<br />I more than once at Noon<br /><br />Have passed I thought a Whip Lash<br />Unbraiding in the Sun<br />When stooping to secure it<br />It wrinkled And was gone -<br /><br />Several of Nature’s People<br />I know, and they know me<br />I feel for them a transport<br />Of Cordiality<br /><br />But never met this Fellow<br />Attended or alone<br />Without a tighter Breathing<br />And Zero at the Bone.<br /></blockquote><br /><br />This book is based on the original editions edited by Mabel Loomis Todd, a neighbor and wife of a local professor, and Thomas Wentworth Higginson, a publisher who knew Dickinson during her life. (It was recently discovered that these two edited out all mentions of “Susan,” Emily’s close friend and sister-in-law.) It’s organized into four sections: Life, Love, Nature, and Time and Eternity. <br /><br />The “Life” section is unfortunately full of fussy little moralisms and nostrums. It’s like she’s trying to write a book of manners and morals for fussy young puritans. Happily, “Love,” and “Nature,” are stronger. The “Time &amp; Eternity” section is focused on death and afterlife, and contains some of her strongest poems, but also a lot of repetitive poems about God and heaven.<br /><br />Note: She uses the word “chrysoprase” often enough that I finally looked it up. Since it was usually used when describing an insect, I thought it was something related to a chrysalis (a reasonable assumption, right?) but it’s not. It’s an apple green gemstone, similar to quartz. Perhaps it was a popular gem back then? I looked up the etymology to see if it’s related to a chrysalis, and I found that “chrysoprase” is from the Greek khrusos (gold) &amp; prason (leek). LOL a gold leek? Ok. I guess it was for the color? And “chrysalis” is just from the Greek for gold. No leeks involved. So they have the same root word but are completely unrelated.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13674380998931770523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2625466731" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2625466731" />
    <a title="Heider Broisler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76975383-heider-broisler"><img alt="Heider Broisler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593694324p2/76975383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2625466731?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heider Broisler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heider Broisler" href="/user/show/76975383-heider-broisler">Heider Broisler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2625466731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7492185791241351812">Poems like Emily Dickinson's makes you question about life and what it means to love. This edition of collected poems gathers simply the most deeply-sentimental poems of love. I loved how the natural symbolism and the strong meanings behind the words worked.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1683266128" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1683266128" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17025129-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583197449p2/17025129.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1683266128?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/17025129-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17025129-trish?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1683266128" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3981349476779911538">This collection was a great introduction to Emily Dickinson's poetry - but I think it was just that, an introduction. While there are certainly some gems within this edition, I found myself uninterested in her poems about nature, which take up a significant part of this book. In time, I could definitely be persuaded to buy a complete collection of Dickinson's poetry to indulge in on a warm spring day. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3046930672" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3046930672" />
    <a title="MonumentToDecency" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51370861-monumenttodecency"><img alt="MonumentToDecency" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466052868p2/51370861.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3046930672?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MonumentToDecency" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MonumentToDecency" href="/user/show/51370861-monumenttodecency">MonumentToDecency</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3046930672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12588917421675639799"><b>While I was fearing it, it came,<br/>But it came with less of fear,<br/>Because that fearing it so long<br/>Had made it almost dear.<br/>There is a fitting a dismay,<br/>A fitting a despair.<br/>'T is harder knowing it is due,<br/>Than knowing it is here.<br/>The trying on the utmost,<br/>The morning it is new,<br/>Is terribler than wearing it<br/>A whole existence through.</b><br/><br/>I've not read much poetry by US writers, actually Benjamin Franklin is about all I've ever really indulged in and that came about out of necessity at university, though I indeed e</span>
  <span id="freeText12588917421675639799" style="display:none"><b>While I was fearing it, it came,<br />But it came with less of fear,<br />Because that fearing it so long<br />Had made it almost dear.<br />There is a fitting a dismay,<br />A fitting a despair.<br />'T is harder knowing it is due,<br />Than knowing it is here.<br />The trying on the utmost,<br />The morning it is new,<br />Is terribler than wearing it<br />A whole existence through.</b><br /><br />I've not read much poetry by US writers, actually Benjamin Franklin is about all I've ever really indulged in and that came about out of necessity at university, though I indeed enjoy his works.<br /><br />My poetry loves lie across the pond in English romanticism. English romanticism conjures all sorts of emotion through vivid imagery and a great command of language, whereas I always felt the US poetry was moreso thematically focused in pioneering, politics, and the new found freedom from British rule. I want love and misery in my poetry not marching and the great chain.<br /><br />I don't know. Both the US and the English romanticism share the majority of themes yet I think I find the English far more relaxed, less defensive. The US romantic individualism, for example, is understandably more aggressive (blunt?) and more politicised whereas the English is just an exploration of being. Can I just add that I find Whitman to be one of the gentler voices in US poetry of the period, and Longfellow and Poe (of course).<br /><br />Then I see an ad for this new Emily Dickinson show. So, as always 'the book is better', and off I go to hunt down her works, which took all of two seconds. Thank you USA (you'll probably never hear me say that again, so savour it). <br /><br />At first I was surprised at the simplicity of Dickinsons style and couldn't see how something so formulaic could be as highly considered as her works are, but therein lies her genius. With the simplest of techniques she constructs outstanding works by relying on the words, often in very short form, to evoke meaning rather than constructing a picture to create meaning.<br /><br />Dickinson's works appeal directly to emotion: we all know what love or melancholy or death is, she doesn't draw us a picture to explain; she simply says what she means and we - as humans with experience of emotion - know precisely of what she writes. I guess it's kind of like being slapped, she just jumps right in, head first. And like a bruise, the more you look, the more you see the brilliant colours, and how they deepen and change each time you look.<br /><br /><b>Hope is the subtle glutton;<br />He feeds upon the fair;<br />And yet, inspected closely,<br />What abstinence is there!<br /><br />His is the halcyon table<br />That never seats but one,<br />And whatsoever is consumed<br />The same amounts remain.</b><br /><br /><b>
  <u>My rating:</u>
</b> 5 preferred miseries out of 5<br /><br />(They're not all miserable but I like the miserable stuff the best.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="12588917421675639799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_179629175" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179629175" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179629175?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781411431935" href="/book/show/7013502-the-collected-poems-of-emily-dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2011">read2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179629175" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12830944789936611950">I've seen a lot of references to Emily Dickinson lately so I decided to give in and read this, which I had downloaded for free from Barnes and Noble last July 4th, when they put up all their volumes in the B&N Classics Series by American authors for free download for Nook or Nook app.<br/><br/>DO read the collected poems of Emily Dickinson. DO NOT read this version. The editors have "helpfully" messed with her stylings, replacing her dashes with other forms of punctuation, ridiculous. You also have to be </span>
  <span id="freeText12830944789936611950" style="display:none">I've seen a lot of references to Emily Dickinson lately so I decided to give in and read this, which I had downloaded for free from Barnes and Noble last July 4th, when they put up all their volumes in the B&amp;N Classics Series by American authors for free download for Nook or Nook app.<br /><br />DO read the collected poems of Emily Dickinson. DO NOT read this version. The editors have "helpfully" messed with her stylings, replacing her dashes with other forms of punctuation, ridiculous. You also have to be slapped in the face with frequent endnotes. Personally, if I come to a word I don't understand, I will try to get the meaning from the surrounding words, or look it up. Don't insult me by being so helpful!<br /><br />I had originally typed up a bunch of quotations from poems I liked but GR ate it, so I will just say that I like Emily Dickinson, but mostly when she is talking nostalgically about books or love. Her high output of poems on death don't really interest me - she is a little obsessive about it, not to mention repetitive. When you read a bunch in a row, the word immortality becomes less clever as a poem ending, and just becomes rather convenient instead. All of her poems read as idealistic, but knowing her life, it isn't too surprising. I am always surprised by how joyful they are, though. Her imaginary experience of the world was quite an ecstatic one. <br /><br />"I dwell in possibility...."</span>
  <a data-text-id="12830944789936611950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_188329629" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/188329629" />
    <a title="Ştefan Bolea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3671630-tefan-bolea"><img alt="Ştefan Bolea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590672330p2/3671630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/188329629?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ştefan Bolea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ştefan Bolea" href="/user/show/3671630-tefan-bolea">Ştefan Bolea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781411431935" href="/book/show/7013502-the-collected-poems-of-emily-dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3671630-tefan-bolea?shelf=ipad">ipad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3671630-tefan-bolea?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer188329629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9478427157274708000">I wasn't impressed by Emily Dickinson -- actually I've read her because Cioran is one of her biggest fans. What I have disliked: a touch of passive nihilism, of Schopenhauerian <i>renunciation</i>. I am not an active spirit myself, but compared to her I am prince Arjuna from Bhagavad Gita (after the conversation with Krishna). Therefore, I would have wished that the intensity of her poems had sparked up her biography as well. But at the same time I realize that the combination between her fire from wit</span>
  <span id="freeText9478427157274708000" style="display:none">I wasn't impressed by Emily Dickinson -- actually I've read her because Cioran is one of her biggest fans. What I have disliked: a touch of passive nihilism, of Schopenhauerian <i>renunciation</i>. I am not an active spirit myself, but compared to her I am prince Arjuna from Bhagavad Gita (after the conversation with Krishna). Therefore, I would have wished that the intensity of her poems had sparked up her biography as well. But at the same time I realize that the combination between her fire from within and complete impersonality and public facelessness (“How dreary to be somebody!/ How public, like a frog …”) turns her in such a splendid case.<br /><br />What I have enjoyed -- the fact that she is such a <i>direct</i> (lyrical) philosopher. She has wonderful poems about anxiety and despair and I am sure (and I am glad) she hasn't read Kierkegaard! She is a true philospher (or better said, a wise woman) and her eyes are so fresh because she doesn't cary the coffin of the history of philosophy (like professional philosophers) after her. She has complete access to immediacy, to directness, to the (essence of the) Real that transgresses ordinary reality. Moreover, she has an aphoristic genius which completes this surreal wisdom.<br /><br />“Power is only pain, <br />Stranded, through discipline”<br /><br />“THE soul unto itself <br />Is an imperial friend,—<br />Or the most agonizing spy <br />An enemy could send.”<br /><br />“Anger as soon as fed is dead; <br />’T is starving makes it fat”<br /><br />“REMORSE is memory awake”<br /><br />“THE brain is wider than the sky, <br />For, put them side by side, <br />The one the other will include <br />With ease, and you beside.” <br /><br />“SOME keep the Sabbath going to church; <br />I keep it staying at home, <br />With a bobolink for a chorister, <br />And an orchard for a dome.”<br /><br />“PROUD of my broken heart since thou didst break it, <br />Proud of the pain I did not feel till thee”<br /><br />“LOVE is anterior to life, <br />Posterior to death, <br />Initial of creation, and <br />The exponent of breath.”<br /><br />“A death-blow is a life-blow to some <br />Who, till they died, did not alive become; <br />Who, had they lived, had died, but when <br />They died, vitality begun.”<br /><br />“ONE need not be a chamber to be haunted, <br />One need not be a house; <br />The brain has corridors surpassing <br />Material place.”<br /><br />“THE difference between despair <br />And fear, is like the one <br />Between the instant of a wreck, <br />And when the wreck has been.”<br /><br />“A thunder storm combines the charms <br />Of Winter and of Hell.”<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9478427157274708000" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10916468933102353219">I'm not a poetry buff - but I suppose if I were to start somewhere, starting with Emily would be an appropriate place. <br/>It was easy to read through the collective works, and I'm itching to own a copy to mark and highlight and dog ear for my own pleasure. It was hard not to do that on my loaned copy for sure. I continually wanted to re-write some of my favorites and plaster them on the walls of my bedroom like an adolescent. <br/><br/>I always imagined Emily to be someone who was exhuberant in life, and was</span>
  <span id="freeText10916468933102353219" style="display:none">I'm not a poetry buff - but I suppose if I were to start somewhere, starting with Emily would be an appropriate place. <br />It was easy to read through the collective works, and I'm itching to own a copy to mark and highlight and dog ear for my own pleasure. It was hard not to do that on my loaned copy for sure. I continually wanted to re-write some of my favorites and plaster them on the walls of my bedroom like an adolescent. <br /><br />I always imagined Emily to be someone who was exhuberant in life, and was quite shocked to find that she was nearly a hermit. In some ways you can feel that through her poems, but there is glimpses of real joy - sort of not what you'd expect of someone who was alone, although maybe not lonely. </span>

<div id="review_2278185998" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2278185998" />
    <a title="Marwa Shafique" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32745288-marwa-shafique"><img alt="Marwa Shafique" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592457011p2/32745288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2278185998?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marwa Shafique" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marwa Shafique" href="/user/show/32745288-marwa-shafique">Marwa Shafique</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2278185998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13488820160376732248"><i>If I can stop one heart from breaking,<br/>I shall not live in vain;<br/>If I can ease one life the aching,<br/>Or cool one pain,<br/>Or help one fainting robin<br/>Unto his nest again,<br/>I shall not live in vain. </i><br/><br/>I have been fascinated with poetry for as long as I remember; I have always had this urge to be one of those individuals who could discuss verses and stanzas that are bustling with complexity and meanings that only someone with a profound knowledge of life would be able to discover. I never thought I had the inte</span>
  <span id="freeText13488820160376732248" style="display:none"><i>If I can stop one heart from breaking,<br />I shall not live in vain;<br />If I can ease one life the aching,<br />Or cool one pain,<br />Or help one fainting robin<br />Unto his nest again,<br />I shall not live in vain. </i><br /><br />I have been fascinated with poetry for as long as I remember; I have always had this urge to be one of those individuals who could discuss verses and stanzas that are bustling with complexity and meanings that only someone with a profound knowledge of life would be able to discover. I never thought I had the intellectual capabilities to be one of them - hence why it took me so long to actually pick up a poetry collection, which has to be one of my deepest regrets in life. But what I don't regret (and who possibly could?) is choosing Emily Dickinson as my first ever dive into the world of poetry, and I knew that the instant I read the above poem. I fell in love - quite literally. I don't think any other poem has touched my heart and soul as much as this has, despite it being one of her more known works. The beauty of these words, weaven so intricately together, however, tell the tale of someone who was kind, genuine, empathetic; someone who knows they can't take away the world's sorrows, but would want to, if given the chance. (which is so incredibly wholesome)<br /><br /><br />Emily Dickinson's poetry is calming - soothing, in fact. You read pages upon pages and feel all your worries fade away into nothingness. I think that was the most beautiful part of my reading experience; whenever I felt distressed beyond compare, I knew I could seek refuge in her poems &amp; find myself in eternal peace. She describes everything with such vigour and wonder that you cannot help but fall deep in the abyss of her words. Being a poetry novice, I do not know what else to say - except share another one of her poems that I truly loved; in hopes that you do too, and decide to pick up this work of art without a second thought. <br /><br /><i>A murmur in the trees to note,<br />Not loud enough for wind;<br />A star not far enough to seek,<br />Nor near enough to find;<br />A long, long yellow on the lawn,<br />A hubbub as of feet;<br />Not audible, as ours to us,<br />But dapperer, more sweet;<br />A hurrying home of little men<br />To houses unperceived, —<br />All this, and more, if I should tell,<br />Would never be believed.<br />Of robins in the trundle bed<br />How many I espy<br />Whose nightgowns could not hide the wings,<br />Although I heard them try!<br />But then I promised ne'er to tell;<br />How could I break my word?<br />So go your way and I'll go mine, —<br />No fear you'll miss the road.</i><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13488820160376732248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17085332705038848277">Despite I found poetry quite complex to understand sometimes, I love reading it and looking for its paraphrase. Emily Dickinson (1830 – 1886) was a fascinating figure and a brilliant poet; she chose to isolate herself from the world but keeping observing it, creating unique themed messages. Life, Nature, Faith, Eternity are mostly the topics of her poems; the peculiarity of her writing lies in describing a vivid introspective identity of the self open to the free interpretation of the reader.<br/><br/>As </span>
  <span id="freeText17085332705038848277" style="display:none">Despite I found poetry quite complex to understand sometimes, I love reading it and looking for its paraphrase. Emily Dickinson (1830 – 1886) was a fascinating figure and a brilliant poet; she chose to isolate herself from the world but keeping observing it, creating unique themed messages. Life, Nature, Faith, Eternity are mostly the topics of her poems; the peculiarity of her writing lies in describing a vivid introspective identity of the self open to the free interpretation of the reader.<br /><br />As a collection of many of her poems I enjoyed some more than others, however I've found her and her style interesting and pleasant.</span>

<div id="review_1311002412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1311002412" />
    <a title="Laura Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15312688-laura-anne"><img alt="Laura Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510871875p2/15312688.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1311002412?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Anne" href="/user/show/15312688-laura-anne">Laura Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517362426" href="/book/show/303865.Collected_Poems_of_Emily_Dickinson">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15312688-laura-anne?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15312688-laura-anne?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15312688-laura-anne?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15312688-laura-anne?shelf=speaks-to-me">speaks-to-me</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1311002412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5069631191057065764">I felt lukewarm about Emily's poetry until the last section of this book in which her famous melancholy side was displayed, then I took quite a fancy to her poems.<br/>Death and Emily Dickinson go together like peanut butter and jelly. I've never known someone to make personal darkness and internal pain into something so beautiful. I especially like how her prose flows from my lips as if begging to be read aloud. <br/>Oh Emily, my favorite hermit. I am so endeared to you.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1066759211" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1066759211" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9698647-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341102729p2/9698647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1066759211?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/9698647-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9698647-matt?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1066759211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12579827343153191446"><i>The Collected Poems of Emily Dickinson</i> contains a sizeable sample of the total works of the reclusive poet, who only came to prominence after her death. Containing 593 poems separated into five different themes, roughly a third of her overall productivity, this collection gives the reader a wonderful look into the talent of a woman who hid her art not only from the world but also her own family. Besides nearly 600 poems of Dickinson’s work, the reader is given a 25 page introduction to the poet </span>
  <span id="freeText12579827343153191446" style="display:none"><i>The Collected Poems of Emily Dickinson</i> contains a sizeable sample of the total works of the reclusive poet, who only came to prominence after her death. Containing 593 poems separated into five different themes, roughly a third of her overall productivity, this collection gives the reader a wonderful look into the talent of a woman who hid her art not only from the world but also her own family. Besides nearly 600 poems of Dickinson’s work, the reader is given a 25 page introduction to the poet and an analysis of her work by Dr. Rachel Wetzsteon who helps reveal the mysterious artist as best as she can and help the reader understand her work better. Although neither Wetzsteon’s introduction and analysis nor Dickinson’s work is wanting, the fact that this collection gives only a sample of the poet’s work is its main and only flaw.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12579827343153191446" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1948969842" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1948969842" />
    <a title="Ella N." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59631924-ella-n"><img alt="Ella N." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473883510p2/59631924.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1948969842?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ella N." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ella N." href="/user/show/59631924-ella-n">Ella N.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Barnes &amp; Noble</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59631924-ella-n?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1948969842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16857011082556888554">This book contains every poem that Emily Dickinson ever wrote. Over the time that I read it, it was actually stolen/mock-stolen and when I retrieved it the thief was pouring over a single poem, trying to reach the hidden meaning. They told me they thought it was about the government, and I thought it was about betrayal in love, yet we both saw the same poem. The actual poem was:<br/>The heart asks pleasure first<br/>And then, excuse from pain.<br/>And then, those little anodynes<br/>that deaden suffering.<br/><br/>And then, </span>
  <span id="freeText16857011082556888554" style="display:none">This book contains every poem that Emily Dickinson ever wrote. Over the time that I read it, it was actually stolen/mock-stolen and when I retrieved it the thief was pouring over a single poem, trying to reach the hidden meaning. They told me they thought it was about the government, and I thought it was about betrayal in love, yet we both saw the same poem. The actual poem was:<br />The heart asks pleasure first<br />And then, excuse from pain.<br />And then, those little anodynes<br />that deaden suffering.<br /><br />And then, if to sleep<br />And then, if it should be<br />The will of the Inquisitor<br />The Liberty to die.<br /><br />Since then I have wondered what it really meant. It was under the "Life" section, if that helps....<br />This book was incredibly deep. Dickinson understood death more than anyone did without dying. She lived in the Victorian Era, when a lot of bad things were going on in everyone's lives. Dickinson did not become famous until after her death, but those who discovered her showed the world a truly gifted artisan who contained the world in her writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16857011082556888554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
